# Miles Gonzalo Morales  —  *Spider-Man*

![[_attachments/miles_morales.png]]

# Miles Morales — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF) — SUBJECT: MORALES, MILES GONZALO

**Classification:** Compiled longitudinal assessment, full-canon scope (2011–present).
**Cross-reference:** See §§ 1–5 (History; Medical; Psychological; Psychiatric; Personality) for substantiation. This Brief is the load-bearing summary; all later sections elaborate, none supersede.

---

## I. BOTTOM LINE

The subject is a securely attached, morally serious adolescent who took a dead stranger's name in an act of guilt-shaped substitution at age thirteen and has, in the decade of recorded canon since, organized his entire existence around the project of *earning a title he believes was conditionally loaned to him*. He is not a damaged person performing wellness; he is a well-formed person performing provisionality. The distinction is the file in one sentence.

Everything operationally interesting about him — the deference, the over-functioning, the rigid no-kill line, the secret-keeping past the point of utility, the visible-to-invisible spectrum of his powers firing on his autonomic state, the chronic permission-seeking from older heroes who keep telling him he does not need permission — is downstream of one fact: **he believes he is provisional, and the people around him keep failing to convince him otherwise.** He will not, on present trajectory, accept his own legitimacy from external testimony. He will only accept it when he gives it to himself, and the canon has not yet shown him doing so.

That is the entire file in compressed form. What follows unpacks it.

---

## II. WHO HE IS AT THE DEEPEST LEVEL

Strip the costume, the bite, the Brooklyn skyline, the dead predecessor, and the inherited name, and what remains is a *child of an intact family who was raised to be good and believed it*. This is rare in the population of costumed subjects, and it is the most important variable in his profile. The standard Marvel etiology — orphaning, abandonment, abuse, ideological recruitment — is absent. He has a mother who loved him out loud and a father who corrected him in detail. He internalized both. His superego is therefore not a punitive intruder but a *familiar voice*; he experiences moral demand the way most people experience hunger — as a baseline signal, not as an emergency.

What this produces, at the deepest level, is a subject whose **default orientation is reparative**. He does not, in his interior, want to win. He wants to *fix*. He wants the room to be okay when he leaves it. He wants the person trying to kill him to also be okay, after, if possible. This is not strategy and it is not pose. It is the shape his ethics took before he had any ethics he could name, and the bite did not alter it; the bite only gave it consequences.

Underneath the reparative orientation sits a quieter, harder structure: a **conviction of contingency**. He believes — and this is observable across every writer, every era, every continuity-shift — that his presence in any given room is *granted* and *revocable*. The charter-school lottery framed it for him in language at age twelve: his uncle said *lucky*; his father said *earned*; he has spent every subsequent year trying to settle the question and has not. He occupies the Spider-Man role the way he occupied that school seat — gratefully, anxiously, with the constant background calculation that someone could legitimately ask him to leave.

The deepest layer, then, is the collision of these two structures: a *reparative ethic* on a *contingent self*. He must constantly help, because that is who he is; he must constantly justify the helping, because he does not believe he is authorized to do it. The result is a subject who works very hard, apologizes for working hard, and cannot stop working hard because stopping would prove the apology correct.

---

## III. THE THROUGH-LINE OF HIS ENTIRE EXISTENCE

One sentence: **a slow, uneven, still-incomplete movement from substituting for Spider-Man to being Spider-Man.**

Read longitudinally, the entire arc is a permission curve, and the curve is shallow. It begins in *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* #1–4 with outright refusal — bitten, terrified, asking Ganke to help him *quit* before he has even begun. It moves to reluctant adoption in *Ultimate Fallout* #4, in the language not of grief but of *complicity-by-absence* ("I could have helped"). It encounters hostile public reception and seeks legitimization from the dead man's surviving women (May Parker, Gwen Stacy), whose blessing produces tears — diagnostic of how he conceives the right to the name as a *bequest from a family not his own*. It is interrupted by the Aaron catastrophe and a year of withdrawal. It is interrupted again by Rio's death and the secret she demanded he keep. It is reset, but not resolved, by the universe-shift restoration of his family. It is interrogated by Civil War II, where he treats a precognitive vision as evidence about his own nature rather than as data about a possible future. It is sublimated into the Champions, where he becomes the team's moral conscience but declines its leadership. It is externalized in the clone arc, where pieces of him literally walk around doing what he fears he might do, and he chooses to take responsibility for them rather than disown them.

Across all of it, **one behavior never changes: he defers to Peter Parker, and Peter Parker keeps telling him he doesn't have to.** The pattern does not move. This is the through-line in miniature. He has been told, by every authority figure who matters, that he is the real thing. He has not yet been able to hear it.

The arc is therefore not tragic and not triumphant. It is *asymptotic*. He approaches the conviction of his own legitimacy without reaching it, and the canonical project — across Bendis, Ahmed, Ziglar, and the *Spider-Verse* satellites — has been to preserve the asymptote rather than collapse it. The writers correctly intuit that the moment he believes he is Spider-Man without qualification, the character ends. So the file documents a subject in *permanent late adolescence on the question of self-authorization*, which is a coherent and stable configuration, not a transitional one.

---

## IV. THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

### The wound

The core wound is **not a trauma**; it is a **structure**. He does not have a wound the way Parker has Uncle Ben or Wayne has the alley. He has, instead, a *recurring confirmation pattern* that his presence is causally entangled with the deaths of people he should have been able to protect. The pattern is empirically false (he did not kill Aaron; Aaron's weapon did. He did not kill Rio; Galactus-adjacent collateral did. He did not kill Peter; the Goblin did, before he had powers). The pattern is phenomenologically inescapable (the people around him keep dying in his vicinity, in his proximity, sometimes in his arms).

The wound is best stated as a proposition he cannot disprove: **"The role I occupy was vacated by a death I did not prevent, and the role I occupy keeps producing deaths I cannot prevent. I am the variable these losses share."** This is survivor guilt with an inheritance topology — guilt not over a single failure but over the *position itself*, which he experiences as cursed and which he experiences himself as having taken voluntarily.

### How the wound is maintained

The wound persists because three independent systems keep refreshing it. Disrupt any one and the structure would weaken; all three are load-bearing across the canon and none have been resolved.

**1. The somatic loop.** His powers fire on his autonomic state. When he is afraid, he disappears; when he is overwhelmed, he discharges current into whatever is touching him. His body *broadcasts his anxiety as physical event*. This means every episode of fear is followed by an episode of *visible-to-others consequence*, which is followed by a fresh episode of guilt for having had the fear at all. He cannot have a private nervous system. The wound is metabolized through the skin, into the world, and back into the self-concept as evidence that he is dangerous when uncomposed. He has responded by developing emotional regulation skills above his age cohort — but the regulation is *defensive*, not integrative, and its failure modes (camouflage = vanish; venom-blast = strike) are the two oldest trauma responses in the human catalog with novel costumed expression.

**2. The secret-keeping protocol.** Initiated tactically (protect family from knowledge), reinforced morally by his mother's dying instruction (*"Don't tell your father"*), it has long since outlived its operational rationale and become an identity practice. He keeps secrets the way devout people keep fasts — as observance. Every concealed truth from his father is a renewed contract with a dead woman who is, in current continuity, no longer dead but whose deathbed instruction he honors anyway because the *somatic memory* of her death survived the universe-shift even when the woman did. He is a man honoring a vow given by a ghost his current mother does not remember being. The secret-keeping is therefore not pathology in the OCD sense — it is *grief work disguised as security protocol*, and it cannot be interrupted by tactical argument.

**3. The inheritance frame.** Peter Parker is alive (616) and accessible, and Miles meets him often. Every encounter reactivates the substitution structure: Peter is the *original*, Miles is the *also*. Peter consistently and explicitly refuses the hierarchy. Miles consistently and implicitly reinstates it. The frame is maintained from below — he keeps choosing it. Adjacent to this, the *Spider-Verse* machinery told him he is an *anomaly* not native to the Web of Life, which he received exactly as he was bound to receive it: as cosmic confirmation of impostor status. The multiverse, in his cognition, has voted, and the vote went against his standing.

These three systems — somatic, ritual, cosmological — converge on the same verdict and refresh the wound continuously. The wound is therefore not a scar; it is a *current*. It is being generated, not remembered.

---

## V. WHAT IS LOAD-BEARING (DO NOT REMOVE)

For any operational read of the subject, the following items are structural. They are not symptoms to be managed; they are joists.

- **The no-kill line.** Not a tactical preference. Not a strong norm. An identity wall. It is the principal evidence he gives himself, daily, that he is not his uncle. Crossing it would not produce remorse — it would produce *collapse*. Any scenario constructed around forcing him across it should be modeled as a scenario that ends the subject as currently configured.
- **The father.** The unresolved disclosure-and-forgiveness axis around Aaron's death is the single highest-amplitude pressure point in the file. His father's prior contempt for Spider-Man, voiced before the disclosure, is encoded as an authoritative moral verdict. Subsequent reconciliation has softened but not dissolved it.
- **The mother's instruction.** Honored across continuities. Treat as standing order he is following, not as memory he is processing.
- **Ganke Lee.** The keystone friendship. Functions as reality-test, decompression chamber, and the one relationship in which he is *not* performing competence. Removing Ganke would not produce a more independent subject; it would produce a more isolated and therefore more dangerous one.
- **Peter Parker as ceiling and floor.** Idealization that will not yield to disconfirmation. He needs Peter to exist as the original so that he can continue being the *also*, which is the only role he currently believes he has earned. Removing Peter — death, departure, fall from grace — would force the self-authorization the canon has so far refused him, and the outcome is unpredictable.
- **Family-as-meaning.** The cape is secondary. Any analysis that treats the costume as the primary unit will misread him. He is a son and a friend who is also Spider-Man, in that order, and the order is not negotiable.

---

## VI. OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

**Disposition.** Reliably benevolent across all observed contexts. No documented action against a non-combatant. No documented lethal action. No documented coercion of an unwilling party. Use-of-force pattern is restraint-first, de-escalatory, *consistently* willing to absorb personal injury to avoid inflicting it.

**Predictability.** High on values, moderate on tactics, low on self-regulation under specific edge cases (forced-choice scenarios; framing-as-uncle; threats to immediate family; public denial of his legitimacy). The edge cases are narrow but sharp.

**Reliability under load.** Inverse-loaded. Performs *better* under catastrophic stress than under moderate stress. His regulation system was built in fire and trusts itself there; in middle ranges he ruminates, sleeps poorly, over-functions, and accumulates concealments. *He is more stable in a crisis than in a Tuesday.*

**Approach vectors (benign).** Direct address; honesty; explicit naming of his competence (he will deflect but will register); patience with deferral patterns; refusal to occupy the authority position he keeps offering. Mentorship works; command does not. Working alongside him is high-yield; working above him is wasted leverage because he will defer past the point of usefulness.

**Approach vectors (adversarial — DO NOT EMPLOY; documented for completeness).** Frame him as his uncle. Force him to choose between two protected persons. Publicly delegitimize his claim to the name in front of his peers. Threaten his father with information his father does not have. Each of these targets a maintenance system of the core wound rather than the subject directly, and each will produce disproportionate effect. Note: these vectors damage *him*, not his function — adversarial use would produce a destabilized but not neutralized subject, and the destabilization could route through the involuntary venom-blast / camouflage axes in ways harmful to bystanders. Tactically unsound as well as ethically prohibited.

**Trajectory.** Toward integration, slowly. The clone arc (taking responsibility for the fragments rather than disowning them) is the strongest evidence to date that he is metabolizing the shadow rather than projecting it. The Champions tenure is the strongest evidence that he can hold a moral position in public without retreating to deference. The unresolved items — full disclosure to his father with the Aaron truth attached; renunciation of the substitution frame with Peter; granting himself the name without qualifier — remain open and may remain open indefinitely. He is the kind of subject whose growth is *real and unfinished*, and the unfinished quality is structural, not deficient.

**Threat assessment to public order.** Negligible. Net positive across every measurable axis.
**Threat assessment to self.** Chronic-low, episodic-moderate. He will continue to take damage he could decline to take, in the service of guilt-discharge framed as duty.
**Threat assessment to those who would harm those he loves.** High, bounded by his no-kill wall. He will go further than he thinks he will, and stop exactly where he said he would stop.

---

## VII. ONE-LINE FILE

A securely loved child who inherited a dead boy's name in an act of guilt, whose body broadcasts his fear, who keeps a vow made by a mother the universe later gave back, and who is still — at the most recent entry in the record — waiting for someone with standing to tell him he is allowed to be what he already, demonstrably, is.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

## Subject: MORALES, MILES GONZALO
### Period covered: Pre-natal through approximately age 12 (i.e., everything prior to the bite of Oscorp Spider #42 in *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* #1, August 2011)

---

## 1.1 — Natality, Parentage, the Brooklyn Substrate

The subject was born in Brooklyn, New York, to Jefferson Davis and Rio Morales. The birth year shifts with the publishing calendar (the floating-timeline convention places him at thirteen in *USM* #1, 2011; he is roughly sixteen–seventeen by current 616 issues), but the natal facts are stable across both continuities. The middle name **Gonzalo** is canonical (*Spider-Man: Miles Morales* Vol. 1 supplementary material; reiterated in the Ahmed run). It is his maternal grandfather's name — a small, important fact in his file because it indicates whose lineage was foregrounded in the act of naming. The father's surname (Davis) was not given to the child. The boy carries the *mother's* surname: **Morales**. This is not a minor administrative detail. It is the first behavioral data point in his life, made on his behalf before he could consent, and it indicates which side of the family was treated as the line of moral inheritance. Jefferson's surname carries Aaron with it; Rio's does not. The Davis brothers' criminal history is encoded in the surname Miles does not bear.

The subject's racial and cultural composition is canonically explicit and behaviorally consequential: Black (paternal, African-American) and Puerto Rican (maternal). The household operates in two linguistic registers — English as default, Spanish as the language of maternal warmth, scolding, and prayer. Rio code-switches into Spanish when emotionally heightened (*USM* #1, the morning of the charter-school lottery, "*ay, mijo*" in moments of pride or fear; pattern repeats across the Bendis run). The subject understands Spanish receptively at near-native fluency, speaks it haltingly and self-consciously — a common second-generation diglossic profile (*Champions* Vol. 2, casual exchange with Sam Alexander, where Miles admits his Spanish is "okay-ish"). This linguistic asymmetry will later become a small but persistent shame point: he can be addressed in his mother's language but cannot always answer in it.

## 1.2 — The Father: Jefferson Davis

Jefferson is the subject's primary daytime parental presence in the early years and the primary axis of expectation throughout childhood. The *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* backstory (issues #2 and #4, and the consolidating retrospective in #28 after Aaron's death) establishes that Jefferson grew up in the same Brooklyn projects as his brother Aaron, ran with Aaron in petty-to-mid-tier criminal work as a young man, was recruited at one point by a S.H.I.E.L.D. operation (Nick Fury, in flashback) to infiltrate Wilson Fisk's organization, and ultimately reformed — distancing himself violently from Aaron after a final break. By the time Miles is born, Jefferson is working blue-collar, possibly security-adjacent (early issues are vague; later issues retcon toward NYPD service in the 616 continuity), and is publicly and privately a man committed to the proposition that *who you choose to be is more important than who your family was*.

This commitment is preached to Miles continuously, in low-grade daily doses, from the earliest period he can remember. The household catechism — observable in clipped form in *USM* #1, then again across *USM* #4, #5, #16 — is roughly: *be better than where we came from; don't trust costumes; don't trust the police; do trust your mother; stay away from your uncle*. These five tenets are non-negotiable in Jefferson's domestic register. They are the moral water Miles swims in for his entire pre-bite life.

Behaviorally, Jefferson runs hot. He is not abusive — there is no canonical evidence of physical discipline or verbal cruelty toward Miles — but he is *declarative*, *binary*, and *loud* on questions he considers settled. He yells at Aaron in front of Miles (*USM* #1, the doorstep confrontation after the bite incident is discovered: Jefferson's voice carries down the hall, Miles listens from inside). He expresses contempt for Spider-Man (the original) in dinner-table monologues (*USM* #16, "men in masks make things worse"). He does not modulate these positions for child audiences; he believes children should be inducted into the truth. This produces in the young Miles a household in which **moral certainty is performed daily by the most important man in the room**. The downstream consequence: Miles develops a deep, early sensitivity to his father's verdicts and an internal default that *Dad's opinions are the world's opinions*. When, years later, Jefferson tells Miles (not knowing he is speaking to Spider-Man) that he hates Spider-Man, the boy does not weigh that statement against other data; he treats it as a fact about the universe (*USM* #28).

## 1.3 — The Mother: Rio Morales

Rio is a nurse. The specific shift pattern shifts across writers but the type is constant: irregular hours, hospital-based, often nights. This has a structural effect on Miles's earliest routines — he is frequently present at his mother's departures and arrivals, sees her in scrubs, registers her exhaustion, and learns early that *caring for strangers' bodies is what adults do for money and meaning*. She is the warmer parent, the affectionate one, the one who kisses the top of his head in public without checking whether it embarrasses him (*USM* #1, the lottery scene; *USM* #200, the final tableau). She is also the religious one — Catholic, Puerto Rican Catholic specifically, with a small home altar and a saint's medal she occasionally tucks into Miles's pocket before he leaves the apartment (*USM* #4, brief panel; *Spider-Man* Vol. 2 #1, Bendis run, the medal is shown again on his nightstand). Miles is not, in himself, devout, but the *iconography* of his mother's faith — saints, prayers, blessings — is part of his earliest sensory record of safety.

Rio's discipline style is the inverse of Jefferson's. Where Jefferson declares, Rio *inquires*. She asks Miles questions about what he was thinking, what he was feeling, what he meant. She forgives quickly and apologizes when she has been wrong (*USM* #1, "I'm sorry I yelled, *mijo*"). The earliest emotional-regulation skill set Miles develops is built on her template: *examine, articulate, repair*. This is the substrate of the above-age affect-regulation he later demonstrates as Spider-Man. He learned it before he was ten, in a kitchen in Brooklyn, from a tired woman in scrubs.

The marriage between Jefferson and Rio is — across virtually all canonical depictions — *functional and warm*. They argue (*USM* #4, the post-bite hospital scene), but they argue in front of him without weaponizing him. They reconcile visibly. They touch each other casually (a hand on a shoulder, a brief kiss before one of them leaves for work). This is, statistically and clinically, **the most stable parental dyad in the front line of the Marvel Universe**. It is the single most important environmental fact in the file. The subject's later capacity to bear unusual loads without fragmenting is built directly on this foundation.

## 1.4 — The Uncle: Aaron Davis

Aaron, Jefferson's younger brother, is the subject's primary *forbidden* relationship from the earliest age at which Miles can remember being told who someone is. The prohibition is explicit and repeated. Miles is told, in some form, *don't go see your uncle*, throughout the period — and the prohibition itself is what creates the relationship. Aaron, when permitted contact (typically family events Jefferson cannot prevent, or chance encounters), is *charismatic* to the child: generous with attention, gift-giving, story-telling, talking to Miles as if he were older than he is. The pre-bite dynamic established in *USM* #1 — Miles slips out to visit Aaron's apartment, alone, against direct paternal instruction — is not the first such visit. It is the latest in an established childhood pattern.

The subject's earliest formation thus includes a **smuggled adult attachment**: a man whose attention is sweet, whose attention is forbidden, and whose forbidden status is justified by adults Miles trusts but cannot quite see violated in Aaron's actual conduct toward him. Aaron, in those early interactions, does not behave badly to Miles — he behaves *too well*, in a way calibrated to undermine Jefferson. This is the precise psychological mechanism of triangulating a child against a parent, and it is the most damaging thing done to Miles in his pre-bite life. It produces a slow-curing skepticism toward parental absolutism (*if Dad is wrong about Uncle Aaron, what else?*) which is the wedge that allows the bite-day visit to occur at all.

The uncle's later betrayal does not erase this early attachment; it *complicates* it, which is worse. Miles will spend years unable to cleanly grieve Aaron and unable to cleanly hate him, because the man who tried to weaponize him was also the man who, when Miles was eight years old, took him to a comic shop and bought him whatever he wanted (*USM* #4, flashback panel; reiterated in the Ahmed run's clone arc, in which the corrupted fragments of Miles speak in cadences closer to Aaron's than to Jefferson's).

## 1.5 — The Household & the Block

The family lives in Brooklyn — Bendis's run places them in a working-class apartment in a building with elevator failures, audible neighbors, and a stoop the family uses (*USM* #4, #5, multiple establishing shots). The 616 relocation eventually moves them to a Brooklyn brownstone-adjacent address, but the *somatic memory* of childhood is the apartment building: shared hallways, a building super, the smell of other people's cooking, the noise of the street as constant ambient. The subject's lifelong comfort with **density, proximity, and overheard life** is built here. He sleeps better with traffic noise than without it. He moves through crowds without flinching. This is a Brooklyn childhood in the specific somatic sense — bodies of strangers always within ten feet, navigated without thought.

The block has a candy store, a bodega, a barbershop, a basketball court within three blocks of the apartment. Miles knows the bodega owner by name (*USM* #5, brief panel — he buys a soda and the owner waves). He has the geography memorized at the granular level a child develops when he has walked the same six-block radius for a thousand days. This map is later the substrate of his Spider-Man patrol patterns: he polices, by preference, the territory he learned as a pedestrian child. His instinct is to *watch his own block first*, and to extend outward reluctantly.

## 1.6 — Ganke Lee and the Pre-Bite Dyadic Bond

Ganke Lee is canonically established as Miles's best friend from before the events of *USM* #1 — they meet in the lead-up to the charter-school lottery period, and the friendship is mature and bantering at first appearance, indicating significant prior history. Ganke is Korean-American, LEGO-obsessed, comic-literate, and emotionally calibrated as Miles's *opposite-in-affect-but-same-in-ethics* partner: where Miles is anxious and inward, Ganke is excitable and outward. He is the first non-family person Miles tells anything important to, and he establishes (before the bite ever occurs) the **dyadic disclosure template** Miles uses for the rest of his life: one trusted peer who knows everything, around whom defensive scaffolding is unnecessary.

The childhood pattern with Ganke is sleepovers, shared LEGO builds, comic discussions (Ganke knows Spider-Man lore in detail — *USM* #4, he is the one who recognizes the spider-bite symptoms because he has read about Peter Parker), and a low-friction familiarity that the subject does not replicate with anyone else until much later. The bond's importance in the file is structural: **Miles enters adolescence already practiced at having one person who knows the truth**. This is why, when secret-keeping becomes a survival pathology, Ganke is the exemption. Ganke was always the exemption.

## 1.7 — School & the Lottery

The subject is academically gifted from early childhood. His parents identify it early; teachers identify it; he is tracked into competitive academic programs throughout elementary school. The canonical apex of this pre-bite period is the **Brooklyn Visions Academy charter lottery**, depicted in the opening pages of *USM* #1 — a boarding-school admission obtained by literal lot, not merit, after merit qualified him for the lottery pool. The scene is the structural origin of his entire psychological file:

- The lottery is won.
- Jefferson tells him: *you earned this.*
- Aaron tells him: *you got lucky.*
- Rio cries.
- Miles, in close-up, does not know which framing to believe.

This is the bifurcated frame — luck versus desert — that defines his self-concept from that moment forward. Before the bite, before the costume, before the dead predecessor and the dead uncle and the dead mother, **the question of whether Miles Morales deserves what he has been given is already the operative question of his interior life**. The bite does not create the wound. The bite finds a wound already configured to receive it.

Behaviorally at school: he is quiet, polite, well-liked but not popular, gravitates toward one or two close friends rather than groups, raises his hand more often than he speaks unprompted, finishes assignments early, reads ahead. Teachers report him as *conscientious* — the word recurs. He is not a class clown; the humor he later deploys as Spider-Man is private, interior, deployed sparingly out loud. In group projects he defaults to the supportive-collaborator role and does more than his share without complaint. This is, in miniature, the Champions-era Miles: useful, modest, over-functioning, deferential.

## 1.8 — Early Behavioral Patterns (Pre-Bite Baseline)

Compiled from observable conduct across the Bendis-era flashbacks, the Ahmed-era memory sequences, and the implied prior-history in *USM* #1:

- **Sleep:** sound, until disrupted by external stress. Sleeps with the door slightly open as a child. Wakes when his mother comes home from a night shift, briefly, then sleeps again.
- **Eating:** unfussy, omnivorous, loyal to his mother's cooking (rice and beans recur as comfort food across the canon; *Champions* Vol. 2 background panel). Snacks excessively at Ganke's. Has no early food aversions of clinical note.
- **Movement:** physically *competent* but not athletically distinguished pre-bite. Average for age. Did not play organized sports. Walked everywhere a Brooklyn child walks.
- **Speech:** moderate volume, full sentences, polite address to adults. Says *please* and *thank you* without prompting (Rio's training). Does not interrupt. Asks before taking. Apologizes promptly and sincerely.
- **Lying:** rare and bad at it. The lies he tells as a child are small (the missed homework, the unauthorized snack) and his face gives him up. This will be relevant later: he becomes a *good* secret-keeper in adolescence not because he becomes a *good liar* but because he becomes practiced at *not speaking*. Omission, not fabrication. This is a meaningful distinction in his file.
- **Conflict avoidance:** strong. Will leave a room rather than escalate. Will absorb a small injustice rather than name it.
- **Animals:** comfortable with them, no documented aversion (relevant given the impending spider event — there is no canonical record of arachnophobia, which makes his post-bite terror about *the powers*, not about *the spider*).
- **Heights:** no documented fear, also relevant.
- **Authority figures:** treats adults with default respect, makes eye contact, answers questions directly. Notably *unintimidated* by male authority — Jefferson's loud register has inoculated him against being startled by raised adult voices — but emotionally *attuned* to adult disapproval, which lands harder than yelling.

## 1.9 — Formative Micro-Traumas (Pre-Bite)

There is no single catastrophic pre-bite trauma. There are, instead, a series of small-to-medium structural strains that pre-configure the later wound:

- **The chronic Aaron prohibition** and its periodic violation, generating early-life shame and triangulation.
- **Overheard parental arguments**, primarily about Aaron, in which Miles learns that adult love is real but does not resolve disagreement.
- **The economic precarity ambient to a nurse-and-blue-collar household in Brooklyn**, not depicted as poverty but as the constant background fact of *attention paid to bills, lights left off when not needed, leftovers honored*. Miles develops early what working-class children develop: an instinct that resources are finite, that waste is a moral failure, that he should not ask for what is not offered.
- **The implicit narrative of his father's reformation**, which Miles assembles in pieces (overheard remarks, Aaron's elliptical comments, eventually direct conversations later in life). He grows up knowing that his father was once *someone he is now ashamed of having been*. This installs early the proposition that **a person can be more than one thing across time** — which is hopeful (people can change) and frightening (which one is the real one?). This proposition will haunt his clone arc.
- **The lottery moment itself**, which is not trauma but *initiation*: the moment at which the question of his own desert becomes conscious and unanswerable.

## 1.10 — Synthesis of the Pre-Bite Substrate

The subject enters the inflection point of his life — the visit to Aaron's apartment in *USM* #1 — as a thirteen-year-old boy with: a secure attachment to both parents; a smuggled, contaminated attachment to a forbidden uncle; one deep peer bond; demonstrated academic capacity; an above-age vocabulary for his own feelings inherited from his mother; an inherited fear of becoming his father's brother inherited from his father; a working-class Brooklyn somatic baseline; no record of clinical pathology; and an unresolved internal question — *did I earn this, or did I get lucky?* — that he is already, at thirteen, carrying in a way most adults never carry anything.

He is, on every measurable dimension, *well-raised*. He is also, on the dimensions that will matter most, **pre-loaded for the exact wound he is about to receive**. The bite is the precipitating event. The childhood is the substrate that determined what kind of hero — and what kind of haunted person — the bite would produce.

The file's first principle, set in this period and never overturned: *Miles Morales was loved into existence by people who told him, in different words, that love does not exempt anyone from the obligation to deserve their own life.* Everything that follows is the working-out of that instruction.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

## Subject: MORALES, MILES GONZALO

### Period covered: ~ages 11 through 14 (pre-bite adolescence through the consolidation of the Spider-Man identity, *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* #1 through approximately *USM* #28; Brooklyn-Manhattan corridor)

---

## 6.1 The Pre-Bite Window (ages 11–13): The Charter School Lottery and the Bifurcated Frame

The file's earliest behavioral footage of the subject as a self-aware actor — as opposed to as a child being acted upon — opens on a wooden chair in a charter-school auditorium, ankles not quite touching the floor, a numbered ball in the lottery cage on a stage in front of him. The scene is reconstructable from *Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* #1 (Bendis/Pichelli, August 2011) and is, for our purposes, the **Rosetta Stone** of the subject's pre-bite formation. Three behaviors register simultaneously:

1. **Posture of containment.** Hands folded, shoulders inward, eyes forward. He does not look at his parents. He has been told, explicitly, that this matters; he is performing the receipt of seriousness.
2. **Asymmetric parental support.** Rio's hand is on his back. Jefferson's hand is on his own knee. The mother contacts; the father witnesses. This becomes a pattern.
3. **The pre-emptive shame response when his number is called.** The subject does not exult. He covers his face. His first expressed reaction to good fortune is the somatic gesture of hiding from it.

In the parking lot afterward, the family encounters Uncle Aaron. The exchange compresses the central interpretive frame of the subject's entire life into three sentences. Jefferson: *"He earned it."* Aaron: *"You got lucky, little man."* Rio, defusing: *"He's smart. Of course he won."* The subject says nothing. He is eleven. He is being told, in real time, by three of the four most important people in his world, that the same event is **either evidence of merit, evidence of randomness, or evidence of innate worth**. He is not asked which he believes.

The subject's later cognitive style — the chronic re-litigation of his own legitimacy, the tendency to weigh prophecy and accusation against character as if both were admissible evidence — is, on the file's reading, **prefigured here**. He grew up in an evidentiary environment where the meaning of his own successes was contested by the adults around him. He learned to leave the verdict open.

### 6.1.a Domestic baseline, ages 11–13

The pre-bite home environment is, by Marvel standards, **anomalously functional**:

- **Apartment, Brooklyn.** Walk-up, modest, kitchen-as-center. Family meals are depicted as routine. Rio cooks; Jefferson sets the table; Miles is expected to clear. (*USM* #1, #4, ancillary panels through #28.)
- **Religious/cultural baseline.** Rio's Puerto Rican Catholicism is present but un-emphasized in the household; Jefferson's stance is secular. There is a crucifix in the kitchen. No formal religious observance is depicted; cultural Catholicism (mother's Spanish in moments of stress, the cross, the saints' iconography in Rio's medical scrubs locker per a *USM* #14 panel) is the residue.
- **Linguistic register.** Miles is functionally bilingual but operates primarily in English at home. Rio uses Spanish under stress and as an endearment vector (*"mijo"* recurrent throughout). Miles understands fluently; speaks haltingly. This asymmetry — receptive bilingual, productive monolingual — is itself diagnostic of a child who absorbs more than he expresses.
- **School performance.** Documented academically gifted. Pre-charter-school placement was a neighborhood public school where he was understimulated and bullied lightly (referenced retrospectively, *USM* #2). The lottery admission to Brooklyn Visions Academy was, in Jefferson's frame, the formal validation of years of his son's quiet effort.

### 6.1.b The Aaron Variable

The single most important pre-bite relational fact in the file is that **Jefferson Davis had told his son Aaron was off-limits**, and the subject had complied for years. The visit that produces the bite is therefore not the casual stop-in it superficially resembles; it is the subject's first deliberate, sustained act of paternal disobedience. The chronology:

- Aaron is the **forbidden charismatic uncle**. He sends gifts on birthdays — sneakers, a watch, once a phone — that Jefferson intercepts. The subject knows about the gifts.
- The subject, age ~12, encounters Aaron on the street near Visions Academy approximately three weeks before the bite event (referenced *USM* #2, in flashback). Aaron does not push. He hands him a card with a phone number.
- Subject keeps the card in a paperback copy of *A Wrinkle in Time* on his bookshelf. (Inferred from a Pichelli detail panel; consistent with subject's documented bibliophilia.)
- The visit occurs on a Friday afternoon. Subject tells his parents he is studying at Ganke's. Ganke is briefed and asked to corroborate. **This is the subject's first documented operational lie.** It is small, social, plausible, and effective. The fact that it works will matter later: the subject's adult-grade secret-keeping competency was rehearsed first against the people he loved most, in a low-stakes context, and rewarded with success.

The visit itself, *USM* #1: Aaron's apartment is messy in the specific way that signals a working criminal — surfaces clear of paper, locks heavy, blinds drawn at 3 PM. The subject registers it without naming it. He is offered juice. He accepts. The spider — Oscorp specimen #42, smuggled out by Aaron in a B&E several days prior — has escaped its containment and is in the room.

---

## 6.2 The Bite and the Forty-Eight Hours After (*USM* #1)

**Bite location:** dorsal aspect, right hand, between the second and third metacarpals. **Time of envenomation:** approximately 4:50 PM, Friday. **Subject's immediate behavioral response:** he looks at his hand, says *"ow,"* crushes the spider with a paperback (which he then drops, which he then picks up and returns to a shelf — the **tidying gesture under acute stress** that recurs throughout his file), and says nothing to his uncle. He completes the visit. He goes home. He eats dinner.

The latency period is, by his own subsequent account, *"a couple of hours."* He collapses in his bedroom and remains unconscious through most of Saturday. Rio attributes it to flu. He awakens Sunday morning with:

- Adhesion (palmar/plantar setae-equivalents) — discovered when his hand sticks to the bathroom door frame
- Enhanced strength — discovered when he crushes a toothbrush
- The first involuntary **camouflage event** — discovered when he looks in the mirror and is not there.

The mirror scene is, on the file's reading, the formative somatic event of his entire psychology. **His first encounter with his new self is an encounter with his own absence.** A subject whose primary novel ability is to *not be visible* — and which fires first under the stress of self-confrontation — is being told something by his own body. The file notes this and moves on.

### 6.2.a The refusal (*USM* #2)

Within seventy-two hours of the bite the subject has done the following:

- Refused to tell his parents.
- Confided in Ganke Lee, who is electrified and immediately frames the situation in **Spider-Man** terms.
- Visibly recoiled from that framing.
- Attempted to **return** the powers — first by sheer denial (refusing to use them), then by approaching Sam Alexander/Nick Fury indirectly via SHIELD-adjacent channels, then by asking Ganke to research how to *get rid of them*.

This refusal lasts months. It is **the most underweighted period in the subject's biography** in pop-cultural reception. He had powers and chose not to use them. He went to school. He did homework. He attended Ganke's *Lego Star Wars* assembly sessions in their shared dorm. He was, by intention, a normal thirteen-year-old who happened to be able to stick to ceilings.

The file's interpretive note: **the subject's heroism is not native.** It is not, as with Parker, the result of a galvanizing personal failure that he then overcorrects against for life. The subject was offered the role, examined it, and declined. What follows — the entire career — is the consequence of having the decision retroactively forced on him by **someone else's death he did not cause**. This is structurally unprecedented in the Spider-canon and dictates everything about how he holds the mantle.

---

## 6.3 The Mantle Assumed in Borrowed Cloth (*Ultimate Fallout* #4)

The death of Peter Parker (*Ultimate Spider-Man* #160, June 2011) occurs during the subject's refusal period. He watches the funeral on television in his dorm room with Ganke; the surveillance-grade detail in the file is that he watches it **alone the second time**, on his laptop, at 2 AM, headphones in, on a loop, for approximately three nights. Ganke does not know this.

The mantle is adopted in *Ultimate Fallout* #4 (August 2011). The costume is a department-store Spider-Man Halloween knockoff — cheap polyester, plastic eye-shells. The first night out he stops a mugging, badly. He is filmed. The footage goes viral. The public reaction is **rage**.

The cognition the file imputes to him at this moment, based on his subsequent reasoning patterns: *I should have been there. I could have helped. I was there, and I did nothing, and now he is dead, and someone has to.* This is **substitution logic**, not vocation logic. He is not stepping into a role he has earned; he is filling a vacancy left by a death he failed to prevent through the simple negligence of **not having been ready when he should have been**.

The choice of the knockoff costume is itself diagnostic. He does not design his own. He does not modify it. He puts on the dead boy's face and goes out to do the dead boy's job. The later adoption of the black-and-red suit — gifted/blessed by Nick Fury after the **May Parker confrontation** — is the first sartorial step toward selfhood, and the file flags it accordingly.

### 6.3.a The May Parker / Gwen Stacy scene (*USM* #4–5)

Approximately six weeks into operation, the subject is confronted at the Parker residence in Queens. May Parker slaps him — once, hard. Gwen Stacy weeps. Then May, having looked at him, **gives him Peter's web-shooters**. The subject, on-panel, weeps openly. The behavioral data here:

- The subject did not defend himself when struck.
- The subject did not speak for the duration of the confrontation until given the shooters.
- The subject's verbal response on receiving them was *"I'll be good. I promise."*

That sentence is the foundational vow of his career. Note the structure: **"I'll be good"** — not "I'll be effective," not "I'll be safe," not "I'll be careful." The promise is **moral, not operational**. He treats the inheritance as a behavioral contract with the bereaved. The contract is binding. He will spend a decade discharging it.

---

## 6.4 The Operational First Year (*USM* #5–21): Calibration

Across the first year of activity the file tracks a steepening competency curve overlaid on a flat-line self-concept. He gets better at the job; he does not get better at believing he should be doing it.

### 6.4.a The Daily Routine (ages 13–14)

Reconstructed from cumulative panels and references across *USM* #5–28:

- **05:30** — Wake. (Earlier than other students; the early-rise pattern persists into 616.)
- **05:30–06:30** — Rooftop reconnaissance run, Brooklyn → Manhattan and back, depending on overnight news. Establishes that he was checking for trouble before homeroom.
- **06:45** — Return to dorm via window. Ganke leaves the latch open. (Ganke's complicity is a load-bearing operational fact; the subject's career is, logistically, impossible without him.)
- **07:00–07:30** — Shower, breakfast (cafeteria; documented preference for cereal and bananas; avoids the eggs).
- **08:00–15:30** — School. Maintains a 3.6+ GPA throughout the bite period despite documented sleep debt.
- **15:30–18:00** — Patrol or homework, prioritized by Ganke's read on the police scanner. Ganke runs an informal dispatch from the dorm.
- **18:00–20:00** — Dinner with family on home weekends; cafeteria on school weekdays.
- **20:00–23:00** — Homework / patrol overlap. Has been observed completing algebra problem sets on rooftops.
- **23:00–01:30** — Patrol.
- **01:30–05:30** — Sleep, when achieved.

The chronic sleep debt of the early period is the file's first medical concern about him and is **never resolved**. He runs sleep-deprived for years. The body compensates via spider-augmented physiology; the cognition does not. Decision quality degrades visibly in the late-night panels (Pichelli draws the eye-bags; Marquez later codifies them).

### 6.4.b The Tells (early period)

Surveillance-grade behavioral micro-patterns established in this first year and stable thereafter:

- **The hand-rub.** Right thumb across the dorsal of the left hand — the bite site. Occurs under stress, particularly social-evaluative stress. He is, neurologically, scratching the scar of the origin event.
- **The pre-swing pause.** Three to five seconds on the launch perch before he goes. Parker swings continuously; the subject **stops between problems**. The pause is not tactical; it is volitional re-commitment. Every patrol contains dozens of these.
- **The headphone tell.** When grieving or processing, he listens to music with one earbud in, one out. He has never been seen with both ears occluded. Operationally this is auditory vigilance; psychologically it is the refusal to fully leave the world.
- **The "Sorry" reflex.** Apologizes to civilians he has not injured. Apologizes to muggers he has webbed. Apologizes to inanimate objects he collides with. The apology rate per patrol hour is, conservatively, an order of magnitude above Parker's.
- **The texted check-in.** Texts Ganke between fights, not after. The communication is not a status report; it is a tether. The pattern is, on the file's reading, **a thirteen-year-old's instinct to call home from the dark**.

### 6.4.c The Spider-Woman intercept (*USM* #6–7)

Jessica Drew (the female-cloned Peter Parker variant) intercepts him on a rooftop and **tells him he has no right to the name**. He does not argue. He absorbs it. He internalizes it. The file's note is that this exchange — between a girl who is *literally* Peter Parker and a boy who is *trying to be* Peter Parker — establishes the public/peer **legitimacy ceiling** he will spend his career trying to break through. Drew later relents; the ceiling does not. He behaves, in subsequent appearances, as though every senior hero he meets retains the right to administer the same judgment.

---

## 6.5 The Aaron Catastrophe (*USM* #22–28)

The defining sequence of the Formation period and the file's primary forensic interest in the early years.

### 6.5.a Discovery

Aaron Davis, operating as the Prowler, **identifies his nephew as Spider-Man** via a combination of physical observation (build, voice, the hand-rub tell) and operational intelligence (knowledge of the Oscorp spider's escape from his own apartment). The recognition is, in the canon, slightly older than the confrontation; he sits on it for weeks, watching. The subject does not detect the surveillance. **This is the first and last time a member of his family knows his identity before he discloses it.** It poisons the disclosure problem for the rest of his life.

### 6.5.b Coercion

Aaron's approach is not threat but **family**. He shows up. He offers training (legitimate; he is genuinely skilled). He offers love (transactional; price to be named later). He offers an alternative father-figure (the unspoken indictment of Jefferson). The subject **accepts the training**. This is the file's most important behavioral data point of the period:

> The subject, knowing his father's prohibition, knowing his uncle's criminality, knowing that the entire setup is wrong, chose proximity to the charismatic uncle over the strictures of the absent father. He did this for the second time. The first time he was bitten by a spider; the second time he was bitten by a worse one.

The training sequences are operationally valuable. He learns. His combat improves measurably. The improvement is **paid for** with complicity in increasingly compromised situations — an "errand" that turns out to be a robbery; a "favor" that turns out to be intimidation.

### 6.5.c The price named

Aaron names the price: **partnership**. Permanent, criminal, lucrative. The subject refuses. Aaron's response is the threat to **expose his identity to Jefferson** — the calculated detonation of the subject's deepest available wound.

### 6.5.d The fight and the death

The combat is detailed across *USM* #27–28. The subject fights without intent to kill. Aaron does not return the courtesy. Aaron's gauntlet — the Prowler's signature weapon, jury-rigged technology — **malfunctions under overload** during a venom-blast exchange and detonates against his own arm. He dies in front of the subject.

**Dying words: *"You're just like me."***

The file's forensic note: the line is **factually false and psychologically fatal**. Aaron's last act was to plant a cognitive contagion in the boy who killed him by accident. The subject will spend years testing the proposition. He will eventually disprove it, behaviorally, by the cumulative evidence of a life lived in the opposite direction. He will never be free of having to disprove it.

### 6.5.e The compounded secrecy

Within forty-eight hours of Aaron's death:

- Jefferson learns Aaron died fighting Spider-Man.
- Jefferson tells Miles, **in conversation, in their kitchen,** that he **hates** Spider-Man, that Spider-Man is a vigilante who killed his brother, that no good can come of people like that.
- Miles is sitting at the table. He is eating cereal. He says *"yeah, Dad."* He clears his bowl. He goes to his room. He does not cry until the door is shut.

The triple-secret structure is now installed: **identity, complicity, contempt.** All three are load-bearing; removing any one collapses the others. He cannot disclose his identity without disclosing his complicity; he cannot disclose his complicity without confirming his father's contempt; he cannot bear his father's contempt without losing the secure base that lets him function at all. **The architecture of the lie is, at this moment, structurally rational.** It will outlive its rationality by years.

### 6.5.f The first retirement

Subject ceases costumed activity. The cessation lasts approximately **one year of in-story time** (*USM* #28 through *Cataclysm*, 2013). Behavioral profile during the hiatus:

- Grades improve.
- Sleep improves.
- Affect flattens.
- He is, by all external markers, **a better-functioning adolescent without the cape**.
- He is also, in private — Ganke documents it without naming it — **diminished**. He does not laugh as readily. He has not put down the burden; he has put down only the activity. The guilt, having no operational outlet, metastasizes inward.

The retirement is broken by an external catastrophe (the *Cataclysm* event) too large to refuse. He does not choose to return so much as the world chooses for him. Note the pattern from the original assumption: **he has now adopted, retired from, and resumed the mantle without ever once having decided, from a position of equilibrium, that he wants the job.** Every transition has been forced by external events. This non-volitional career structure is the file's central explanation for his lifelong impostor cognition. **A man who never chose the role cannot fully believe he holds it.**

---

## 6.6 Consolidation of the Operational Self (end of Formation period)

By the close of the period under review — approximately the subject's fourteenth year, end of *USM* #28 — the following structures are **set** and will persist into 616 with high fidelity:

1. **The substitution frame.** I am here because someone else is not.
2. **The borrowed-mantle frame.** The role is conditional, contingent on the approval of the dead and the bereaved.
3. **The contaminated-inheritance frame.** I am the nephew of a criminal and may be one myself; the evidence is open.
4. **The triple-secret architecture** with respect to his father, structurally rational at installation, increasingly costly thereafter.
5. **The somatic coupling** of fear to camouflage and anger to venom-blast, with all the downstream emotional-regulation consequences detailed in §3.
6. **The operational deference posture** toward senior heroes (Parker analogues, Cap, Drew, Fury), installed in the first six weeks and never fully shed.
7. **The Ganke node** — the single off-grid relationship through which the subject's secret-life logistics flow, and without which the entire architecture would have collapsed in week three.
8. **The tidying gesture, the hand-rub, the pre-swing pause, the headphone tell, the reflex apology, the tether-text** — the surveillance-grade somatic signature.

What is **not yet** set, and will be the work of the next chronological volume:

- The disclosure to Jefferson (deferred for years; eventual; transformative).
- The death of Rio (*USM* #200; the second forced re-entry into mantle).
- The Civil War II self-prosecution.
- The Champions co-founding and the first reluctant exercise of peer-leadership.
- The clone reckoning and the formal externalization of the *"you're just like me"* contagion into figures he can examine and choose not to kill.
- The 616 relocation and the somatic-memory-versus-current-reality split with his restored mother.

The Formation period closes, in the file's reading, with the subject **functional, competent, secret-laden, and provisional**. He is fourteen years old. He has buried an uncle he killed and lied about it to a father he loves. He has put on and taken off and put back on a name he does not believe belongs to him. He has learned that his body broadcasts his fear and that his fear is, structurally, the most dangerous thing about him. He has acquired, in Ganke, the one human being in his life to whom he does not have to lie, and he has installed that relationship as the load-bearing element of his ability to continue.

He is, by any reasonable measure, **a child who has done extraordinarily well under conditions no child should be asked to operate under**. The file's recommendation, were this a living subject and were intervention available, would have been the same in 2012 as it would be today: *tell your father, and find a therapist who will hold the room while you do.* The subject would not, at fourteen, have been able to hear it. The Formation period closes with that intervention unmade and the architecture of the adult self already poured and curing.

— *end Chronology II*


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Subject's operational lifespan, roughly mid-*Ultimate Comics: Spider-Man* through the *Spider-Verse*/*Spider-Geddon*/Champions/Ahmed-solo arcs. The middle period for SM-II is compressed in real-time (≈2012–2022 publication, ≈3 in-universe years) but is, in psychological mileage, his longest stretch: this is where the borrowed mantle becomes worn-in, where the secret-keeping system reaches structural completion, and where every behavioral tic catalogued in the prior sections becomes hardened from improvisation into character.*

---

## A. The Stabilization Phase (post-debut → Aaron's death)

### A.1 Operational settling, *USM* #5–21

After the unauthorized debut in the Parker-knockoff costume and the public humiliation that followed (Spider-Woman dressing him down on a rooftop; the J.J.J. analog editorials; civilians shouting "take it off"), the subject enters his first sustained operational period in the black-and-red suit gifted by Nick Fury. The change of costume registers as more than wardrobe: it is the first externally-conferred *permission* he accepts. The behavioral signature shifts noticeably in the issues that follow — patrol routes become longer, swing-confidence increases, the involuntary camouflage activations decrease in frequency. He is, briefly, *being* Spider-Man rather than impersonating one.

Pattern data from this window:
- **Patrol geography**: tightly circumscribed to Brooklyn (Sunset Park, Park Slope, Red Hook, DUMBO waterfront), with occasional incursions into lower Manhattan via the bridges. He swings the bridges rather than the skyline — a tell that he is still uncomfortable above twenty stories without a clear vertical surface within reach. Parker by contrast lived in the canopy; the subject lives at the cornice line.
- **Time-on-task**: roughly 10pm–2am on school nights, longer on weekends. Returns home via the fire escape; never the window directly. The detour is a security ritual but also a *cool-down loop* — he uses the rooftop minutes to come down from the sympathetic spike before facing his mother.
- **Ganke as ground-control**: the friendship moves from peer-confidant to functional operations cell. Ganke runs scanner feeds, fabricates costume repairs, alibis him to Rio. The relationship is the first of his lifelong *dyadic dependency* patterns — never a network, always a single trusted node.

### A.2 The Scorpion / Kingpin / Omega Red arcs

The subject's first encounters with named adult criminals are notable for what he *doesn't* do. He does not taunt. He does not name them. He does not declare anything. The Parker-style verbal performance is almost entirely absent in this window; his combat vocalizations are short, often apologetic ("sorry — sorry —" mid-throw is documented twice). When he webs Omega Red unconscious he stares at the body for a beat before leaving; the panel is silent. This is the first appearance of what becomes a lifelong tic: the *post-takedown pause*, a moment of moral inventory before swing-off, never present in Parker.

### A.3 First Avengers contact

Fury's recruitment overture and Captain America's wary endorsement function as external validation he does not know how to receive. His response is overcompliance: he attends briefings, takes assignments, defers to seniority, and never asks for compensation, transit help, or training he obviously needs. The pattern — *accept the legitimacy conferred, refuse the support it entitles you to* — locks in here and persists through Champions and beyond.

---

## B. The Aaron Catastrophe and the Year of Silence

### B.1 *USM* #22–28: the lethal climax

The subject's uncle, having identified him under the mask, escalates from coercion ("we could *run* this city, little man") through emotional blackmail (threats to disclose to Jefferson) to physical attack. The combat that ends Aaron's life is brief, ugly, and — in the panel-by-panel — *initiated by Aaron's weapon malfunction, not by Miles*. This factual detail is critical for the file because the subject does not register it that way. In every subsequent reference (interior monologue, dream sequences, the Champions debrief on Civil War II), he frames Aaron's death as *something he did*. The discrepancy between the evidentiary record and the subject's autobiographical record is the single most reliable indicator of his guilt-processing pathology: he edits causality inward.

Aaron's terminal utterance — *"You're just like me"* — is encoded permanently. It surfaces verbatim or in paraphrase across at least seven subsequent canonical moments through 2022. He has not metabolized it. He has filed it as evidence.

### B.2 The withdrawal (*USM* #28 → *Cataclysm* prologue, ≈ in-universe 13 months)

The subject *quits*. The costume is boxed and stored under his bed. He attempts a normal-adolescent reconstitution: more time with Ganke, a brief relationship with Katie Bishop, increased schoolwork. The reconstitution does not take. Surveillance-grade behavioral indicators across this period:
- **Sleep architecture degraded**: Ganke reports him awake at 3–4am repeatedly. Subject claims homework.
- **Avoidance of Aaron-associated geography**: he refuses to cross 4th Avenue between 36th and 45th for months. Detours documented. Never explained to family.
- **Reflexive flinch to police sirens** developed in this window and never lost.
- **Subject begins lying to his father about small things** — where he was, who he was with — even on days when no Spider-Man activity is occurring. The secret-keeping infrastructure, having been built for one purpose, has become a default cognitive style. This is the most dangerous downstream consequence of the Aaron arc and the one most under-discussed in his own narration.

### B.3 The Katie Bishop disclosure-and-betrayal

Subject's first attempt to *solve* the disclosure problem with a romantic partner. Outcome catastrophic: Katie is HYDRA-adjacent via her father; she attempts to turn him in. The lesson he extracts is not the correct one ("vet your disclosures") but the disabling one ("disclosure is the failure mode"). For the remainder of his romantic history — Sanchez, the brief Bishop overture, subsequent partners — he never again volunteers identity without external pressure forcing it. The pattern: *trust is offered, identity is withheld, relationship erodes under the withholding, relationship ends, he blames himself, repeat*.

---

## C. *Cataclysm*, *USM* #200, and the Mother-Wound

### C.1 Reactivation under Galactus

The reactivation is involuntary in the moral sense — Galactus's arrival forecloses the option of staying retired. The subject re-suits without ceremony, an act he later describes (to Ganke) as "I didn't decide, I just had it on." The phrasing is diagnostic: he experiences the resumption as something that *happened to him*, not as a choice. This will become his standard frame for every subsequent escalation of his hero role.

### C.2 Rio's death (*USM* #200)

The most consequential single event in his life, with the possible exception of the bite itself. The collateral damage of the Venom/Galactus events kills his mother in his arms. She recognizes him under the mask in her terminal seconds. Her final instruction — *"Don't tell your father"* — converts the secret-keeping from a self-protective tactic into a *posthumous filial obligation*. This is the moment the compartmentalization becomes load-bearing, not optional. Disclosing to Jefferson now requires disobeying a dying mother. He will not do that for over a year of in-universe time.

Somatic and behavioral signatures across the immediate post-Rio period:
- **Appetite collapse** for ~3 weeks; Ganke and Jefferson both note weight loss.
- **Speech reduction**: per Bendis's scripting, his dialogue word-count drops by approximately half across the issues following her funeral.
- **Increased involuntary camouflage activations** — at school, mid-conversation, twice in front of teachers who do not register what they have seen. The subject has lost the regulation he had built; the powers are once again broadcasting his nervous system.
- **Compulsive over-help**: he begins responding to *every* scanner call, exhausting himself. The over-functioning is unmistakable reparation behavior.
- **Cessation of mirror-checking**: per a single revealing Ganke line, the subject stops looking in mirrors for a stretch. The shadow-self anxiety has acquired a literal somatic refusal.

### C.3 The Secret Wars hinge

The universe-shift that folds him into the 616 continuity returns Rio alive but does not return *the version of him that did not lose her*. His operative psychological state through the entirety of the post-*Secret Wars* period is that of a son who watched his mother die and now sees her at breakfast. He never resolves this on-panel because no one — including, apparently, himself — knows how to script it. The behavioral consequence is a chronic, low-grade *waiting* affect that is recognizable across every subsequent appearance: he hugs his mother slightly too long; he calls home more than other teenagers; he checks his phone for her texts during combat. The over-protection of family that defines his middle-period heroic ethic begins here, not in any explicit decision.

---

## D. Integration into the Main Universe

### D.1 The dual-Parker problem

In the 616, his predecessor is *not dead*. This is, for the subject, a profound recalibration. The mantle he believed he was holding in trust is in fact still being held by its original owner. His response is structurally revealing: rather than feeling displaced, he feels *relieved* — Peter Parker's continued existence partially refunds his sense of legitimacy debt. The cost is that he now has a living elder to defer to, and he defers extensively. The Peter-Miles dyad across the *Spider-Men II* and subsequent crossovers is characterized by:
- Miles habitually positions himself a half-step behind Peter in combat, even when his speed/agility outclass Peter's reaction time.
- Miles asks Peter's permission for things Peter has no authority over (his college choice, in one strip).
- Peter repeatedly tells him to stop. He does not stop.
- When Peter is injured, Miles's distress response is disproportionate — comparable in intensity to his response to Ganke being hurt.

The dynamic is *older-brother idealization*, not mentor-protégé. Peter is functioning as the elder Miles never had — the uncle the uncle wasn't.

### D.2 School, neighborhood, civilian re-rooting

In 616 he is enrolled at Brooklyn Visions Academy (boarding-adjacent, allows him to live with Ganke during the week and at home on weekends). The split-domicile arrangement is *operationally convenient* and *psychologically suspicious*: it gives him institutional cover for nocturnal absences and minimizes parental observation of his physical condition. He does not appear to have arranged this consciously, but he does not move to change it. The pattern of *accepting structural conditions that enable secret-keeping* is consistent.

Patrol territory expands in this era to encompass parts of lower Manhattan, with Brooklyn remaining the home turf. He develops a small number of repeated swing-paths — the Manhattan Bridge cable run, the Atlantic Avenue corridor, the Prospect Park perimeter — that function as both efficient routes and *self-soothing geography*. He swings them when not on call.

---

## E. *Civil War II*: The Prophecy and the Self-Verdict

### E.1 The vision

Ulysses's precognition depicts the subject standing over a dead Captain America on the Capitol steps. The subject's response, as flagged in the shared assessment, is not denial but interrogation. He treats the vision as *forensic evidence about his own future nature* rather than as a contested data point. This is the cleanest diagnostic episode in his canon for the *outsourcing of moral self-knowledge* pattern.

### E.2 The Rogers conversation

He goes to Steve Rogers and presents himself for judgment. The encounter is, on Rogers's side, gentle and dismissive (the vision is contested, the future is malleable, etc.). On the subject's side it is *confessional* in structure — he behaves as a penitent before a confessor. He asks Rogers what to do *if it turns out to be true*. Rogers has no answer; nobody could. The subject leaves the encounter no more settled than he arrived but having performed the ritual of submission to elder authority. This is the *form* of his moral processing throughout the middle period: when in doubt, present self to a senior figure for adjudication.

### E.3 Downstream effects

For months following the vision, his combat behavior becomes measurably more *restrained*. Strike force calibrated downward, venom-blasts more sparing, civilian-extraction prioritized over combatant-engagement. He is, in effect, trying to behaviorally falsify the prophecy. This is functional in the short term but cements his already-strong tendency to under-leverage his power profile, with downstream consequences for his Champions tenure.

---

## F. The Champions

### F.1 Founding

The walkout from the Avengers (with Ms. Marvel and Nova) is, on paper, his most assertive political act of the middle period. In practice, the decision is Kamala's; the subject signs on without hesitation but without origination. His role across the entirety of the Champions tenure is consistent and load-bearing: he is the *de-escalator*, the *moral thermometer*, and the *consensus-builder*. He almost never proposes the direction. He frequently proposes the restraint.

### F.2 Operational pattern within the team

- **Position in formation**: defensive/extractive. He pulls civilians; others engage the principal threat. This is partly powers-fit (his venom-blast is short-range, his camouflage is exfiltration-coded) and partly a behavioral preference.
- **Vote pattern in team decisions**: votes against violent solutions in a documented majority of contested moments. Votes against expulsions, against unilateral actions, against confrontations with senior heroes.
- **Conflict-resolution function**: when Kamala and Sam Alexander argue, Miles mediates. When Viv Vision and Riri Williams clash on tactics, Miles mediates. He is rarely the *party* to internal conflict; he is almost always the *third* who reconciles the first two. This is recognizable as a sibling-dynamic role — the child of intact parents who keeps the peace at the family table.
- **Affective labor**: he checks in on teammates. Disproportionate share of "you okay?" panels across the team's run are his.

### F.3 The cost

The Champions tenure cements an *over-functioning under-claiming* equilibrium that he carries into every subsequent affiliation. He works harder than required for less recognition than warranted and registers no complaint. The pattern is gratifying to those around him and corrosive over time to him. Sleep debt across the Champions years is chronically elevated. Ganke is replaced as primary confidant by Kamala for emotional matters, while Ganke retains the logistics/handler role — the first time his confidant-system has supported two nodes.

### F.4 Outlawed / mid-period operational hardening

The Outlawed arc (post–Kamala's Law) is the first sustained period in which the subject operates under *active state hostility*. His response: comply with the form of the law (reduced public-facing activity, increased camouflage use during patrol) while refusing its substance (continued operations). The pattern of *quiet defiance within apparent compliance* will recur. He is not built for open insurgency; he is built for *unobserved continuation*. The camouflage power becomes, in this period, his most-used ability — a fact whose psychological symbolism is on-the-nose.

---

## G. *Spider-Verse* / *Spider-Geddon*: The Anomaly Verdict

### G.1 The Web of Life

Across the multiversal arcs, the subject is repeatedly identified as *anomalous* — not native to the Web, an extra, a duplicate, a problem. The Inheritors' threat profile treats him as a target of opportunity rather than a totemic spider proper. He internalizes this taxonomy with terrible efficiency. The recurring framing — *you are not supposed to exist here* — is metabolized as confirmation of the impostor anxiety he has carried since *USM* #5.

### G.2 Behavior in multiversal company

In rooms full of Spider-figures, the subject's deferential tic becomes pronounced to the point of being commented on by other Spiders. He laughs at others' jokes more than he tells his own. He volunteers for the dangerous extraction missions. He gravitates toward the variant Spiders who are *youngest* or *most peripheral* — the Anya Corazons, the Gwens, the Peni Parkers — and toward the elder figures who can serve as additional Peter-substitutes. He never sits at the center of a council shot. Photographic evidence across multiple events is consistent: he is in the second ring, never the first.

### G.3 The bond with Gwen Stacy (Spider-Gwen / Ghost-Spider)

The cross-dimensional friendship with Gwen Stacy stabilizes into the most psychologically *symmetric* peer relationship he has — both are young, both inherited their mantle in conditions of compressed grief, both have a parent who has explicitly disapproved of their costumed activity. He can be more honest with her than with anyone in his home dimension precisely *because* she does not share his civilian life and therefore cannot leak. The relationship is romantically charged but tactically guarded. Recurring pattern: he initiates emotional disclosure with her and aborts it before it lands. The aborted-disclosure tic — opening a sentence, stopping, claiming nothing — is one of his most reliable middle-period behavioral signatures.

---

## H. The Ahmed Solo Era: Confronting the Shadow

### H.1 The clone arc (Selim, Shift, Mindspinner et al.)

The introduction of clones — fragments of the subject with his powers and what he experiences as his uncle's instincts — is the externalization of the *am-I-Aaron* anxiety that has structured him since *USM* #28. The arc's narrative provides what the subject has not been able to provide for himself: literal, embodied evidence that the *bad Miles* and the *good Miles* are separable entities, and that his own intactness is not an accident but a *choice repeatedly made*.

His response to the clones is diagnostic. He does not seek to destroy them. He seeks to *take responsibility for them*. Selim in particular he tries to integrate, to teach, to redeem. This is the reaction-formation pattern operating at full strength: confronted with externalized evidence of his shadow, he chooses adoption over repudiation. The choice is morally admirable and psychologically incomplete — he is still treating the shadow as a *moral problem to solve* rather than a *part of self to integrate*. The book ends without full integration; this is honest.

### H.2 Assembly of Evil and the second tier of antagonists

The Ahmed run also gives him his first *personal* rogues — antagonists keyed specifically to his pathologies rather than inherited from Parker's bench. The Assembly's recurring tactical structure (multiple coordinated mid-tier threats forcing prioritization decisions) targets exactly his weakest decision-axis: the forced-choice scenario. He resolves these encounters with extreme physical cost and minimal civilian loss. The pattern of *absorbing the cost into his own body to spare the civilian calculus* is now load-bearing.

### H.3 Civilian-life pressure increasing

Across the Ahmed run, his family/civilian life is given increased weight. The arrival of his baby sister Billie shifts the family system. The subject moves from *protected child* to *protective older brother* in his self-conception. He begins routing his patrol planning around her schedule — home for bedtime when possible, the Brooklyn-perimeter swing-path scheduled around afternoons she is in the park. The over-protection that began with Rio's death generalizes to Billie with no transition.

### H.4 The disclosure to Jefferson — partial, then complete

The eventual disclosure to his father is the middle period's most important corrective act. The build-up is years long; the act itself is anticlimactic, which is its own diagnostic point — when the subject finally does the thing he has feared for the entire arc, the consequences are bearable. Jefferson is shaken, angry, frightened, ultimately loving. The relationship is not destroyed. The lesson, properly extracted, would be: *disclosure is survivable*. Whether the subject has fully extracted it is unclear. He continues to default to compartmentalization in new relationships even after the success of this one. The pattern is durable past its evidentiary basis.

---

## I. Evolution of Patterns Across the Middle Period

A longitudinal read of the behavioral indicators across this stretch yields the following trajectory:

- **Camouflage usage**: increases over time, from emergency-only in the early *USM* to routine operational tool by the Champions/Outlawed era. The power becomes the *signature* of his style — invisibility-and-strike rather than confrontation. Symbolically, his preferred mode of operation is *to not be seen*.
- **Venom-blast calibration**: improves over time. Early period is binary (off or maximum); by the Ahmed era he is modulating strength on the fly. The neurological coupling to fear has been substantially trained out, replaced by deliberate triggering. This is the cleanest evidence of *acquired regulation* in his profile.
- **Post-takedown pause**: persists throughout, never lost.
- **Apology-frequency mid-combat**: persists, slightly reduced over time. Still verbalizes "sorry" to opponents he is incapacitating, particularly to first-time/young/scared antagonists.
- **Mirror-avoidance**: episodic, spikes after losses, never fully resolves.
- **Phone-checking frequency for family**: increases steadily. By the Ahmed era he checks for Rio's texts in flight.
