**Fork 6: Does he choose the Champions over the Avengers when both are available?** The choice is symbolic but diagnostic. Champions = peer-cohort, ethics-forward, his own generation. Avengers = elder-deference, legitimacy-by-association, the institutional inheritance. He has chosen Champions to date. Drift toward the Avengers — particularly Avengers leadership — would indicate that the impostor-anxiety has been resolved by *institutional* validation rather than *internal* validation, which would look like growth but is actually a sophisticated form of the same defense. A genuinely integrated Miles would be able to occupy either room without the choice meaning anything about his worth.

**Fork 7: Does he tolerate a long stretch of nothing going wrong?** Underrated fork. The subject has never had eighteen consecutive months of stability. If he gets it, the question is whether he can inhabit it without manufacturing crises to discharge anticipatory dread. Growth: he learns to rest. Catastrophe: he develops a low-grade restlessness that selects, unconsciously, for high-risk operational choices that produce the crises he expects.

---

### 6.5 The Aging Question

Most costumed subjects age slowly in published time and rapidly in subjective canon-time, producing a characteristic decoupling. The subject is interesting because his arc is *constituted* by adolescence — refusal, deference, permission-seeking — and the natural resolution of much of his pathology is simply *growing up*. The structural risk is that editorial reluctance to age him will hold him at the developmental stage where his pathologies are functional narrative engines. A perpetually-seventeen Miles is a Miles who *cannot* integrate; the integration *is* aging. Forward assessment must therefore acknowledge that the subject's growth ceiling is partly a function of forces external to his psychology.

If allowed to age: the prognosis is genuinely good. By his late twenties he is one of the more psychologically intact senior costumed subjects in the field — not because his wounds heal, but because his attachment base is robust enough to carry them. By his late thirties, with disclosure complete and a stable partnership, he is the figure other young Spiders defer to, and he extends to them the permission he never quite gave himself, which is a recognizable and benevolent form of partial integration.

If held in stasis: the pathologies stabilize as character. The impostor-anxiety becomes humility-as-affect rather than humility-as-symptom. The secret-keeping becomes operational hygiene rather than displaced loyalty to a dying mother. The quiet erosion proceeds.

---

### 6.6 What Would Push Him

**Toward growth:**
- A father who survives, learns the truth, and stays.
- A peer (most plausibly Khan) who consistently treats him as equal rather than as protégé or charge.
- A younger Spider-figure who needs him to be the elder.
- A romantic partner who knows everything and is not destroyed by it.
- A sustained interval without catastrophic loss.
- A single moment of being publicly, unambiguously called *the* Spider-Man by a figure whose authority he cannot dismiss — and his nervous system not flinching.
- A practice (journaling, dialogue, therapy) that externalizes the interior so it stops running as background process.
- Aaron, somehow, posthumously contextualized — by Jefferson, by a surviving relative, by recovered material — as a man who did real harm rather than as a curse-line.

**Toward catastrophe:**
- Loss of Jefferson, particularly before disclosure completes.
- Loss of Ganke, whose function in his life is undervalued and structurally irreplaceable.
- A forced-choice scenario with public visibility.
- A clone atrocity he cannot disprove or undo.
- An authoritative external verdict (vision, classification, accusation from a trusted elder) that he is dangerous.
- A long-running mentorship from Peter that never converts to peerage — slow poison rather than acute crisis.
- Editorial perpetuation of his adolescence past the point at which the developmental tasks are still age-appropriate.
- Cosmic-scale narrative events that literalize his anomaly status.

---

### 6.7 Bottom-Line Forecast

The subject is, on balance, one of the better-prognosis costumed adolescents in the field. His secure attachment, intact ethical floor, robust friendships, and unusually sophisticated affect regulation give him a baseline that most of his peers lack. He is unlikely to fall. He is also unlikely, on the current trajectory, to *rise* — meaning to occupy the integrated, self-authorized version of himself that his canon has been gesturing toward for over a decade. He will continue to do the work to a high standard; he will continue to do it on probation.

The single highest-leverage intervention available is the completion and stabilization of the disclosure to his father. Everything else routes through it. The single highest catastrophe risk is the loss of that same father before that work completes.

He will, in all likelihood, neither break nor fully heal. He will keep going. He will keep helping. He will at some point, probably in his mid-twenties, look back at the boy who put on Parker's costume in *Ultimate Fallout* #4 and understand that the borrowed thing became his somewhere along the way without his noticing. Whether he lets himself believe it is the open question of the file.

— UATU, watching.

## Relationship Graph
### [[Peter Benjamin Parker (Spider-Man)]]
- Predecessor whose death installed Miles in the role; survivor-guilt template
- Senior peer in 616, maturing past pure mentorship into something like brotherhood
- Trust: very high. Safety: high. Failure mode: if Peter ever publicly endorsed putting the name down, or conversely demanded Miles 'be more like him,' Miles would either collapse or rupture - the legitimacy axis runs through Peter and is not closed
- Leverage: anything framed as 'what Peter would have done' moves him; anything framed as Peter being disappointed in him destabilizes him; threat to Peter triggers operational tunnel vision

### [[Jefferson Davis (Jefferson Morales)]]
- Father, present and loving, with concealed criminal past and later S.H.I.E.L.D. work - the original protective-liar Miles learned from
- Trust: bedrock. Safety: high but not absolute - the relationship survives concealment by mutual unspoken contract
- Failure mode: full transparency event (identity exposure + Aaron contact + lying ledger all surfacing at once) ruptures the household; Jefferson's history of complicated loyalties means a wrong turn could pit father against son operationally
- Leverage: invoke Jefferson's voice and Miles slows down; threaten Jefferson and Miles abandons rules of engagement (short of killing); ask Miles to lie to Jefferson and he will, then hate himself for it

### [[Rio Morales]]
- Mother, the regulatory anchor; Puerto Rican household-warmth pole
- Trust: bedrock. Safety: highest in the cast. Twice-vulnerable in Miles's mind since the Ultimate-era death and restoration
- Failure mode: any threat to Rio overrides all other considerations; her death (re-death) would likely trigger another quitting arc and a deeper depressive collapse than the first
- Leverage: her cooking, her voice, her Spanish in the kitchen all downshift his nervous system; her disappointment is the heaviest weight in the inventory

### Billie Morales
- Baby sister, post-Ahmed run; pure protective-obligation focus
- Trust: total. Safety: he is her safety, not the reverse
- Failure mode: harm to Billie would push him past restraints that have held against every other pressure
- Leverage: anything threatening Billie is operational priority one

### [[Aaron Davis (The Prowler)]]
- Uncle, the Prowler; the unhealed limb. Forbidden by Jefferson, source of the bite-apartment, killed by Miles in Ultimate continuity, resurrected/recontacting in current 616
- Trust: contaminated. Safety: low. Genuine bond layered over genuine harm
- Failure mode: Aaron is the single most reliable destabilizer in the cast - any contact reopens origin wound, paternal-rule violation, kill-guilt, and protective-lying spiral simultaneously
- Leverage: an actor who can credibly route a message through Aaron has access to Miles's deepest unprocessed material

### [[Ganke Lee (Ganke)]]
- Best friend, roommate, identity-aware from day one - one of the healthiest non-romantic bonds in the Spider-corpus
- Trust: total. Safety: high. The relational anchor most likely to reach him during decompensation
- Failure mode: if Ganke were ever weaponized against him - turned, replaced, killed - Miles loses his primary regulator and the protective-lying structure has nowhere safe to release pressure
- Leverage: Ganke is the single point through which Miles can be reliably reached when withdrawn; correspondingly, the single point whose compromise is operationally catastrophic

### Kamala Khan Ms Marvel
- Champions co-founder, peer of nearest temperament - shared earnestness, shared legacy-anxiety, shared faith-in-coalition
- Trust: very high. Safety: high
- Failure mode: if Champions project failed catastrophically, both would internalize blame; ideological rupture between them would shake Miles's coalition worldview
- Leverage: appeals framed as the Champions' integrity move both of them in tandem

### Champions Cohort
- Peer network and ethical-formation context post-Civil War II
- Trust: high. Safety: real but role-distributed
- Failure mode: youth-coalition fractures along adult-institutional pressure lines (the recurring template); Outlawed-era stress
- Leverage: pressure on the team is pressure on Miles's organizing project, which is pressure on his identity

### Romantic Partners
- Barbara Rodriguez, Starling, others by arc - earnest, anxious, secret-hollowed
- Trust: extended fast, retracted reluctantly. Safety: variable
- Failure mode: secret-keeping erodes the bond before disclosure happens; relationships end in guilt-residue rather than clean rupture
- Leverage: a partner who pressed disclosure could either force growth or trigger withdrawal - high-volatility lever

### Shield Avengers Adult Institutions
- Suspect but not enemies - wants better institutions, not none
- Trust: low. Safety: situational
- Failure mode: an institutional order to cross his ethical line (kill, surveil peers, betray a young hero) produces polite refusal and quiet counter-organizing
- Leverage: institutional appeals must be framed as protecting specific named people, not as compliance with authority
